<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41588"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Origin and evolution of the octoploid strawberry genome"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Genetics 2019 51:3"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2019-02-25"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2019 The Author(s), under exclusive licence to Springer Nature America, Inc."/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Cultivated strawberry emerged from the hybridization of two wild octoploid species, both descendants from the merger of four diploid progenitor species into a single nucleus more than 1 million years ago. Here we report a near-complete chromosome-scale assembly for cultivated octoploid strawberry (Fragaria&#8201;&#215;&#8201;ananassa) and uncovered the origin and evolutionary processes that shaped this complex allopolyploid. We identified the extant relatives of each diploid progenitor species and provide support for the North American origin of octoploid strawberry. We examined the dynamics among the four subgenomes in octoploid strawberry and uncovered the presence of a single dominant subgenome with significantly greater gene content, gene expression abundance, and biased exchanges between homoeologous chromosomes, as compared with the other subgenomes. Pathway analysis showed that certain metabolomic and disease-resistance traits are largely controlled by the dominant subgenome. These findings and the reference genome should serve as a powerful platform for future evolutionary studies and enable molecular breeding in strawberry. Chromosome-scale assembly for the cultivated octoploid strawberry (Fragaria &#215; ananassa) uncovers the origin and evolutionary processes that shaped this complex allopolyploid, providing a useful resource for genome-wide analyses and molecular breeding."/>

Mower</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff10">10</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="University of Nebraska" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0004 1937 0060, grid.24434.35, Center for Plant Science Innovation, University of Nebraska, Lincoln, NE, USA" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Kevin_L_-Childs" data-author-popup="auth-Kevin_L_-Childs">Kevin L.             <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-6498-5409"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0001-6498-5409</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff3">3</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="University of California–Davis, Davis" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0004 1936 9684, grid.27860.3b, Department of Plant Sciences, University of California–Davis, Davis, California, USA" /></span></sup> </li></ul>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Cultivated strawberry emerged from the hybridization of two wild octoploid species, both descendants from the merger of four diploid progenitor species into a single nucleus more than 1 million years ago. Here we report a near-complete chromosome-scale assembly for cultivated octoploid strawberry (<i>Fragaria</i> <i>×</i> <i>ananassa</i>) and uncovered the origin and evolutionary processes that shaped this complex allopolyploid. We identified the extant relatives of each diploid progenitor species and provide support for the North American origin of octoploid strawberry. We examined the dynamics among the four subgenomes in octoploid strawberry and uncovered the presence of a single dominant subgenome with significantly greater gene content, gene expression abundance, and biased exchanges between homoeologous chromosomes, as compared with the other subgenomes. Pathway analysis showed that certain metabolomic and disease-resistance traits are largely controlled by the dominant subgenome. These findings and the reference genome should serve as a powerful platform for future evolutionary studies and enable molecular breeding in strawberry.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

            <svg aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xlink:href="#global-icon-download"/></svg>
        </a>
    </div>
    

                        
                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The cultivated garden strawberry (<i>Fragaria</i> × <i>ananassa</i>), an allo-octoploid (2<i>n</i> = 8<i>x</i> <i>=</i> 56), has a unique natural and domestication history, originating as an interspecific hybrid between wild octoploid progenitor species approximately 300 years before present<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Duchesne, A.-N. Histoire Naturelle des Fraisiers Contenant les Vues d’Économie Réunies à la Botanique, et Suivie de Remarques Particulières sur Plusieurs Points qui ont Rapport à l’Histoire Naturelle Générale, par M. Duchesne Fils. (Didot le Jeune, Paris, 1766)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1033">1</a></sup>. The genomes of the progenitor species, <i>Fragaria virginiana</i> and <i>Fragaria chiloensis</i>, are the products of polyploid evolution: they were formed by the fusion of and interactions among genomes from four diploid progenitor species (that is, subgenomes) approximately 1 million years before present<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Njuguna, W., Liston, A., Cronn, R., Ashman, T.-L. &amp; Bassil, N. Insights into phylogeny, sex function and age of Fragaria based on whole chloroplast genome sequencing. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 66, 17–29 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1043">2</a></sup>. Whereas two of the diploid progenitor species have been identified<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Tennessen, J. A., Govindarajulu, R., Ashman, T.-L. &amp; Liston, A. Evolutionary origins and dynamics of octoploid strawberry subgenomes revealed by dense targeted capture linkage maps. Genome Biol. Evol. 6, 3295–3313 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1047">3</a></sup>, the other two diploid progenitor species have remained unknown. Moreover, the history of events leading to the formation of the octoploid lineage and the evolutionary dynamics among the four subgenomes that restabilized cellular processes after ‘genomic shock’<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="McClintock, B. The significance of responses of the genome to challenge. Science 226, 792–801 (1984)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1051">4</a></sup> in allopolyploids remain poorly understood. Here, we present what is, to our knowledge, the first chromosome-scale assembly of an octoploid strawberry genome, the identities of the extant diploid progenitor species of each subgenome, and novel insights into the collective evolutionary processes involved in establishing a dominant subgenome in this highly polyploid species.</p><p>The Rosaceae are a large eudicot family including a rich diversity of crops with major economic importance worldwide, such as nuts (for example, almonds), ornamentals (for example, roses), pome fruits (for example, apples), stone fruits (for example, peaches), and berries (for example, strawberries)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Folta, K. M. &amp; Gardiner, S. E. Genetics and Genomics of Rosaceae (Springer, New York, 2009). " href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1058">5</a></sup>. Strawberries are prized by consumers, largely because of their complex array of flavors and aromas. The genus <i>Fragaria</i> was named by the botanist Carl Linnaeus, on the basis of the Latin word ‘fragrans’, meaning ‘sweet scented’, describing its striking, highly aromatic fruit<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Staudt, G. Taxonomic studies in the genus Fragaria typification of Fragaria species known at the time of Linnaeus. Can. J. Bot. 40, 869–886 (1962)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1065">6</a></sup>. A total of 22 wild species of <i>Fragaria</i> have been described, ranging from diploid (2<i>n</i> = 2<i>x</i> <i>=</i> 14) to decaploid (2<i>n</i> = 10<i>x</i> <i>=</i> 70)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Liston, A., Cronn, R. &amp; Ashman, T.-L. Fragaria: a genus with deep historical roots and ripe for evolutionary and ecological insights. Am. J. Bot. 101, 1686–1699 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1091">7</a></sup>. The genus <i>Fragaria</i> is highly interfertile between and within ploidy levels, thus leading to the natural formation of higher-polyploid species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Bringhurst, R. S. &amp; Khan, D. A. Natural pentaploid Fragaria chiloensis-F. vesca hybrids in coastal california and their significance in polyploid Fragaria evolution. Am. J. Bot. 50, 658–661 (1963)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1099">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Milne, R. I. &amp; Abbott, R. J. Reproductive isolation among two interfertile Rhododendron species: low frequency of post-F1 hybrid genotypes in alpine hybrid zones. Mol. Ecol. 17, 1108–1121 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1102">9</a></sup>.</p><p>Polyploid events, also known as whole-genome duplications, have been an important recurrent process throughout the evolutionary history of eukaryotes and have probably contributed to novel and varied phenotypes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Soltis, P. S. Ancient and recent polyploidy in angiosperms. New Phytol. 166, 5–8 (2005)." href="#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1109">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Freeling, M. &amp; Thomas, B. C. Gene-balanced duplications, like tetraploidy, provide predictable drive to increase morphological complexity. Genome Res. 16, 805–814 (2006)." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1109_1">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Doyle, J. J. et al. Evolutionary genetics of genome merger and doubling in plants. Annu. Rev. Genet. 42, 443–461 (2008)." href="#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1109_2">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Van de Peer, Y., Mizrachi, E. &amp; Marchal, K. The evolutionary significance of polyploidy. Nat. Rev. Genet. 18, 411–424 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1112">13</a></sup>. Polyploids are grouped into two main categories: autopolyploids and allopolyploids, involving either a single or multiple diploid progenitor species, respectively<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Stebbins, G. L. Jr. Types of polyploids; their classification and significance. Adv. Genet. 1, 403–429 (1947)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1116">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Comai, L. The advantages and disadvantages of being polyploid. Nat. Rev. Genet. 6, 836–846 (2005)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1119">15</a></sup>. Many crop species are allopolyploids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Leitch, A. R. &amp; Leitch, I. J. Genomic plasticity and the diversity of polyploid plants. Science 320, 481–483 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1123">16</a></sup>, thus contributing to the emergence of important agronomic traits such as spinnable fibers in cotton<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Paterson, A. H. &amp; Wendel, J. F. Unraveling the fabric of polyploidy. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 491–493 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1127">17</a></sup>, diversified morphotypes in <i>Brassica</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Osborn, T. C. The contribution of polyploidy to variation in Brassica species. Physiol. Plant. 121, 531–536 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1133">18</a></sup>, and varied aroma and flavor profiles in strawberry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Ulrich, D. &amp; Olbricht, K. Diversity of volatile patterns in sixteen Fragaria vesca L. accessions in comparison to cultivars of Fragaria×ananassa. J. Appl. Bot. Food Qual. 86, 37–46 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1138">19</a></sup>. Allopolyploids face the challenge of organizing distinct parental subgenomes—each with a unique genetic and epigenetic makeup shaped by independent evolutionary histories—residing within a single nucleus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Comai, L. The advantages and disadvantages of being polyploid. Nat. Rev. Genet. 6, 836–846 (2005)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1142">15</a></sup>. Previous studies have proposed, as part of the ‘subgenome dominance’ hypothesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Schnable, J. C., Springer, N. M. &amp; Freeling, M. Differentiation of the maize subgenomes by genome dominance and both ancient and ongoing gene loss. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 4069–4074 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1146">20</a></sup>, that the establishment of a single dominant subgenome may resolve various (epi)genetic conflicts in allopolyploids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Thomas, B. C., Pedersen, B. &amp; Freeling, M. Following tetraploidy in an Arabidopsis ancestor, genes were removed preferentially from one homeolog leaving clusters enriched in dose-sensitive genes. Genome Res. 16, 934–946 (2006)." href="#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1150">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Freeling, M. et al. Fractionation mutagenesis and similar consequences of mechanisms removing dispensable or less-expressed DNA in plants. Curr. Opin. Plant. Biol. 15, 131–139 (2012)." href="#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1150_1">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Edger, P. P. et al. Subgenome dominance in an interspecific hybrid, synthetic allopolyploid, and a 140-year-old naturally established neo-allopolyploid monkeyflower. Plant Cell 29, 2150–2167 (2017)." href="#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1150_2">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Bottani, S., Zabet, N. R., Wendel, J. F. &amp; Veitia, R. A. Gene expression dominance in allopolyploids: hypotheses and models. Trends Plant Sci. 23, 393–402 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1153">24</a></sup>. However, understanding of the underlying mechanisms and ultimate consequences of subgenome dominance remains largely incomplete<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Bird, K. A., VanBuren, R., Puzey, J. R. &amp; Edger, P. P. The causes and consequences of subgenome dominance in hybrids and recent polyploids. New Phytol. 220, 87–93 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1157">25</a></sup>.</p><p>Subgenome-level analyses in most allopolyploid systems are greatly hindered by the inability to confidently assign parental gene copies (that is, homoeologs) to each subgenome, owing to both large-scale chromosomal changes and homoeologous exchanges that shuffle and replace homoeologs among parental chromosomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lysak, M. A., Cheung, K., Kitschke, M. &amp; Bureš, P. Ancestral chromosomal blocks are triplicated in Brassiceae species with varying chromosome number and genome size. Plant Physiol. 145, 402–410 (2007)." href="#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1164">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Xiong, Z., Gaeta, R. T. &amp; Pires, J. C. Homoeologous shuffling and chromosome compensation maintain genome balance in resynthesized allopolyploid Brassica napus. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 7908–7913 (2011)." href="#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1164_1">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chalhoub, B. et al. Early allopolyploid evolution in the post-Neolithic Brassica napus oilseed genome. Science 345, 950–953 (2014)." href="#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1164_2">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Edger, P. P., McKain, M. R., Bird, K. A. &amp; VanBuren, R. Subgenome assignment in allopolyploids: challenges and future directions. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 42, 76–80 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1167">29</a></sup>. Octoploid strawberry still has a complete set of homoeologous chromosomes from all four parental subgenomes, thus greatly simplifying homoeolog assignment. Furthermore, gene sequences from extant relatives of the diploid progenitor species, which probably still exist for octoploid strawberry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Tennessen, J. A., Govindarajulu, R., Ashman, T.-L. &amp; Liston, A. Evolutionary origins and dynamics of octoploid strawberry subgenomes revealed by dense targeted capture linkage maps. Genome Biol. Evol. 6, 3295–3313 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1171">3</a></sup>, can be used to accurately assign homoeologs to each parental subgenome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Edger, P. P., McKain, M. R., Bird, K. A. &amp; VanBuren, R. Subgenome assignment in allopolyploids: challenges and future directions. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 42, 76–80 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1175">29</a></sup>. However, a high-quality reference genome for the octoploid is needed to fully exploit strawberry as a model system for studying allopolyploidy as well as to provide a platform for identifying biologically and agriculturally important genes and applying genomic-enabled breeding approaches<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Collard, B. C. Y. &amp; Mackill, D. J. Marker-assisted selection: an approach for precision plant breeding in the twenty-first century. Phil. Trans. R. Soc. Lond. B 363, 557–572 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1179">30</a></sup>. The assembly of the octoploid strawberry genome, with an estimated genome size of 813.4 Mb, has been particularly challenging because of its high heterozygosity and ploidy level<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Hirakawa, H. et al. Dissection of the octoploid strawberry genome by deep sequencing of the genomes of Fragaria species. DNA Res. 21, 169–181 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1183">31</a></sup>. For example, the most recently published version of the octoploid strawberry genome is highly fragmented, with more than 625,000 scaffolds, and largely incomplete, with less than 660 Mb assembled after removal of the numerous gaps<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Hirakawa, H. et al. Dissection of the octoploid strawberry genome by deep sequencing of the genomes of Fragaria species. DNA Res. 21, 169–181 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1188">31</a></sup>. Thus, that version of the genome, owing to its overall highly fragmented nature, has not been a useful resource for genome-wide analyses including the discovery of molecular markers for breeding.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Assembly and annotation of the octoploid strawberry genome</h3><p>Our goal was to obtain a high-quality reference genome for the <i>Fragaria</i> × <i>ananassa</i> cultivar ‘Camarosa’, one of the most historically important and widely grown strawberry cultivars worldwide. We sequenced the genome through a combination of short- and long-read approaches, including Illumina, 10X Genomics, and PacBio, totaling 615-fold coverage of the genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM3">1</a>). Illumina (455-fold coverage) and 10X Genomics (117-fold coverage) data were assembled and scaffolded with the software package DenovoMAGIC3 (NRGene) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM4">2</a>), which has recently been used to assemble the allotetraploid wheat (<i>Triticum turgidum</i>) genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Avni, R. et al. Wild emmer genome architecture and diversity elucidate wheat evolution and domestication. Science 357, 93–97 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1220">32</a></sup>. We further scaffolded the genome to chromosome scale by using Hi-C data (401-fold coverage) in combination with the HiRise pipeline (Dovetail) (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM1">1</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM1">3</a>), then performed gap-filling with 43-fold-coverage error-corrected PacBio reads with PBJelly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="English, A. C. et al. Mind the gap: upgrading genomes with Pacific Biosciences RS long-read sequencing technology. PLoS One 7, e47768 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1230">33</a></sup> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM5">3</a>). The total length of the final assembly is 805,488,706 bp, distributed across 28 chromosome-level pseudomolecules (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#Fig1">1</a>) and representing ~99% of the estimated genome size, on the basis of flow cytometry measurements. A genetic map for <i>Fragaria</i> <i>×</i> <i>ananassa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Davik, J. et al. A ddRAD based linkage map of the cultivated strawberry, Fragaria×ananassa. PLoS One 10, e0137746 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1249">34</a></sup> was used to correct any misassemblies, and comparisons to <i>Fragaria vesca</i> were used to identify homoeologous chromosomes.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Collinearity of the diploid and octoploid strawberry genomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Collinearity of the diploid and octoploid strawberry genomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0356-4/MediaObjects/41588_2019_356_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0356-4/MediaObjects/41588_2019_356_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="711" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Macrosyntenic comparison of the entire <i>Fragaria</i> × <i>ananassa</i> and diploid <i>F. vesca</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Edger, P. P. et al. Single-molecule sequencing and optical mapping yields an improved genome of woodland strawberry (Fragaria vesca) with chromosome-scale contiguity. Gigascience 7, 1–7 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1273">37</a></sup> genomes, with each homoeologous chromosome set colored according to its diploid progenitor species (<i>F. vesca</i> in red, <i>F. nipponica</i> in purple, <i>F. iinumae</i> in blue, and <i>F. viridis</i> in green). Details are provided in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM1">8</a>. <i>F. vesca</i> and <i>F. ananassa</i> chromosomes are shown on the <i>y</i> axis and <i>x</i> axis, respectively. <b>b</b>, Gene-retention patterns among the four homoeologous copies of chromosome 1, with color coding as in <b>a</b>. The relative distance along the <i>F. vesca</i> chromosome is shown on the <i>x</i> axis with the total number of analyzed genes. The percentage of genes retained is shown on the <i>y</i> axis, as estimated with sliding windows of 100 genes. The chromosomes of <i>F. vesca</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Edger, P. P. et al. Single-molecule sequencing and optical mapping yields an improved genome of woodland strawberry (Fragaria vesca) with chromosome-scale contiguity. Gigascience 7, 1–7 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1324">37</a></sup> are named Fvb1 through Fvb7. <b>c</b>, A microsyntenic comparison of a region on chromosome 1 between diploid <i>F. vesca</i> and the four homoeologous regions in <i>Fragaria</i> × <i>ananassa</i>. Gray lines indicate shared syntenic gene pairs, and relative orientation is shown in blue (forward) or orange (reverse). The four subgenomes of <i>Fragaria</i> × <i>ananassa</i> are labeled with corresponding diploid species names of potential origins.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We annotated 108,087 protein-coding genes along with 30,703 genes encoding long noncoding RNAs (lncRNAs), which were subdivided into 15,621 long intergenic noncoding RNAs, 9,265 antisense overlapping transcripts (AOT-lncRNAs), and 5,817 sense overlapping transcripts (SOT-lncRNAs) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM6">4</a>). Gene annotation and genome-assembly quality were evaluated with the Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs v 2 (BUSCO)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Simão, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1355">35</a></sup> method (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM1">5</a>). Most (99.17%) of the 1,440 core genes in the embryophyta dataset were identified in the annotation, thus supporting a high-quality genome assembly. The repetitive components of the nuclear genome were annotated with a custom-repeat-library approach<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Campbell, M. S. et al. MAKER-P: a tool kit for the rapid creation, management, and quality control of plant genome annotations. Plant Physiol. 164, 513–524 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1362">36</a></sup>, including DNA transposons, long-terminal-repeat retrotransposons (LTR-RTs; for example, <i>Copia</i> and <i>Gypsy</i>), and non-LTR retrotransposons (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM1">6</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM1">4</a>). Transposable element (TE)-related sequences make up ~36% of the total genome assembly, and LTR-RTs are the most abundant TEs (~28%). The plastid and mitochondrial genomes were also assembled, annotated, and verified for completeness (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM1">5</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Origin of octoploid strawberry</h3><p>Using the <i>Fragaria</i> <i>×</i> <i>ananassa</i> reference-genome assembly, we sought to identify the extant diploid relatives of each subgenome donor<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Edger, P. P. et al. Single-molecule sequencing and optical mapping yields an improved genome of woodland strawberry (Fragaria vesca) with chromosome-scale contiguity. Gigascience 7, 1–7 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1399">37</a></sup>. Previous phylogenetic studies aimed at identifying these progenitor species, often analyzing a limited number or different sets of molecular markers, have obtained inconsistent results<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Tennessen, J. A., Govindarajulu, R., Ashman, T.-L. &amp; Liston, A. Evolutionary origins and dynamics of octoploid strawberry subgenomes revealed by dense targeted capture linkage maps. Genome Biol. Evol. 6, 3295–3313 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1403">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Potter, D., Luby, J. J. &amp; Harrison, R. E. Phylogenetic relationships among species of Fragaria (Rosaceae) inferred from non-coding nuclear and chloroplast DNA sequences. Syst. Bot. 25, 337–348 (2000)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1406">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Yang, Y. &amp; Davis, T. M. A new perspective on polyploid Fragaria (strawberry) genome composition based on large-scale, multi-locus phylogenetic analysis. Genome Biol. Evol. 9, 3433–3448 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1409">39</a></sup>. However, <i>F. vesca</i> has long been suspected to be a progenitor, on the basis of meiotic chromosome pairing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Fedorova, N. J. Crossability and phylogenetic relations in the main European species of Fragaria. Compil. Natl Acad. Sci. USSR. 52, 545–547 (1946)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1417">40</a></sup>; subsequent molecular phylogenetic analyses supported it being one of the diploid progenitors along with <i>Fragaria iinumae</i> and two additional unknown species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Tennessen, J. A., Govindarajulu, R., Ashman, T.-L. &amp; Liston, A. Evolutionary origins and dynamics of octoploid strawberry subgenomes revealed by dense targeted capture linkage maps. Genome Biol. Evol. 6, 3295–3313 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1424">3</a></sup>. We sequenced and de novo assembled 31 transcriptomes of every described diploid <i>Fragaria</i> species, which we used to identify progenitor species on the basis of the phylogenetic analysis of 19,302 nuclear genes in the genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#Fig2">2</a>, Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM1">6</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM1">8</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM1">7</a>). To our knowledge, this is the most comprehensive molecular phylogenetic analysis of the genus <i>Fragaria</i> to date, including the greatest number of molecular markers and sampling of diploid species, aimed at identifying the extant relatives of the progenitor species of octoploid strawberry (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM1">9</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM1">8</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="The evolutionary history of the octoploid strawberry."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: The evolutionary history of the octoploid strawberry.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0356-4/MediaObjects/41588_2019_356_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0356-4/MediaObjects/41588_2019_356_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="684" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>North-polar projection of present day. Geographic distributions of extant relatives of the diploid (2×) progenitors of <i>Fragaria</i> × <i>ananassa</i>, the putative intermediate tetraploid (4×) and hexaploid (6×) progenitors of <i>Fragaria</i> × <i>ananassa</i>, and extant wild octoploid (8×) species in North America. The colors associated with each diploid progenitor are as in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#Fig1">1</a>. Map data were obtained from Google Maps (see URLs).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Our phylogenetic analyses provided strong genome-wide support for the two diploid progenitor species that had been previously hypothesized and identified the two previously unknown diploid progenitors. This discovery, together with the geographic distributions, natural history, and genomic footprints of the diploid species, provided a model for the chronological formation of intermediate polyploids that culminated in the formation of the octoploid (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#Fig2">2</a>). Our phylogenetic analyses revealed <i>F. iinumae</i> and <i>Fragaria nipponica</i> as two of the four extant diploid progenitor species, both of which are endemic to Japan and in geographic proximity to all five described tetraploid species in China. The third species identified in our analyses, <i>Fragaria viridis</i>, is geographically distributed in Europe and Asia, and partially overlaps with the sole hexaploid species, <i>Fragaria moschata</i>. Therefore, we hypothesized that these tetraploid and hexaploid species may be evolutionary intermediates between the diploids and the wild octoploid species. This possibility is supported by a previous phylogenetic analysis identifying <i>F. viridis</i> as a possible parental contributor to both <i>F. moschata</i> and the octoploid event<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Lundberg, M. Systematics and Polyploid Evolution in Potentilleae (Rosaceae). PhD thesis, Stockholm University (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1503">41</a></sup>. Finally, we identified <i>F. vesca</i> subsp. <i>bracheata</i>, which is endemic to the western part of North America, spanning Mexico to British Columbia, as the fourth parental contributor. Our species sampling also included two other <i>F. vesca</i> subspecies: <i>F. vesca</i> subsp. <i>vesca</i>, which is distributed from Europe to the Russian Far East, and <i>F. vesca</i> subsp. <i>californica</i>, which is endemic to the coast of California.</p><p>Octoploid strawberry species are geographically restricted to the New World and are largely distributed across North America, with the exception of isolated <i>F. chiloensis</i> populations in Chile and the Hawaiian Islands<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Johnson, A. L., Govindarajulu, R. &amp; Ashman, T.-L. Bioclimatic evaluation of geographical range in Fragaria (Rosaceae): consequences of variation in breeding system, ploidy and species age. Bot. J. Linn. Soc. 176, 99–114 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1535">42</a></sup>. Therefore, our phylogenetic analyses combined with the geographic distributions of extant species not only support a North American origin for the octoploid strawberry but also suggest that <i>F. vesca</i> subsp. <i>bracheata</i> was probably the last diploid progenitor species to contribute to the formation of the ancestral octoploid strawberry. This possibility is further supported by a previous study revealing <i>F. vesca</i> subsp. <i>bracheata</i> as the likely maternal donor of the octoploid event, on the basis of the phylogenetic history of the plastid genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Njuguna, W., Liston, A., Cronn, R., Ashman, T.-L. &amp; Bassil, N. Insights into phylogeny, sex function and age of Fragaria based on whole chloroplast genome sequencing. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 66, 17–29 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1552">2</a></sup>. This finding is consistent with our analysis of the plastid genome of ‘Camarosa’ (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM1">10</a>). Thus, these data suggest that the hexaploid ancestor probably crossed into North America from Asia and hybridized with native populations of <i>F. vesca</i> subsp. <i>bracheata</i>, an event dated at ~1.1 million years before present<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Njuguna, W., Liston, A., Cronn, R., Ashman, T.-L. &amp; Bassil, N. Insights into phylogeny, sex function and age of Fragaria based on whole chloroplast genome sequencing. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 66, 17–29 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1565">2</a></sup>. Our phylogenetic analysis also identified related diploid species possibly arising from ancient hybridization and introgression events with putative progenitor species or issues related to incomplete lineage sorting and/or missing data (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM1">6</a>). Future studies will be able to more thoroughly investigate these possibilities after reference quality genomes are assembled for these other diploid progenitor species.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Subgenome dominance in allopolyploids</h3><p>After most ancient allopolyploid events, one of the subgenomes, commonly referred to as the ‘dominant’ subgenome, emerges with significantly greater gene content and more highly expressed homoeologs (that is, postpolyploidy duplicate genes) than those of the other ‘submissive’ subgenome(s)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Thomas, B. C., Pedersen, B. &amp; Freeling, M. Following tetraploidy in an Arabidopsis ancestor, genes were removed preferentially from one homeolog leaving clusters enriched in dose-sensitive genes. Genome Res. 16, 934–946 (2006)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1581">21</a></sup>. Biased fractionation, which results in greater gene content of the dominant subgenome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Sankoff, D., Zheng, C. &amp; Wang, B. A model for biased fractionation after whole genome duplication. BMC Genomics 13 (Suppl. 1), S8 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1585">43</a></sup>, was first described in the model plant <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Thomas, B. C., Pedersen, B. &amp; Freeling, M. Following tetraploidy in an Arabidopsis ancestor, genes were removed preferentially from one homeolog leaving clusters enriched in dose-sensitive genes. Genome Res. 16, 934–946 (2006)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1591">21</a></sup> and later described in <i>Zea mays</i> (maize)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Schnable, J. C., Springer, N. M. &amp; Freeling, M. Differentiation of the maize subgenomes by genome dominance and both ancient and ongoing gene loss. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 4069–4074 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1598">20</a></sup>, <i>Brassica rapa</i> (Chinese cabbage)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Wang, X. et al. The genome of the mesopolyploid crop species Brassica rapa. Nat. Genet. 43, 1035–1039 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1606">44</a></sup>, and <i>Triticum aestivum</i> (bread wheat)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Eckardt, N. A. Genome dominance and interaction at the gene expression level in allohexaploid wheat. Plant Cell 26, 1834 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1613">45</a></sup>. The dominant subgenome has also been shown to be under stronger selective constraints<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Parkin, I. A. P. et al. Transcriptome and methylome profiling reveals relics of genome dominance in the mesopolyploid Brassica oleracea. Genome. Biol. 15, R77 (2014)." href="#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1617">46</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Cheng, F. et al. Epigenetic regulation of subgenome dominance following whole genome triplication in Brassica rapa. New Phytol. 211, 288–299 (2016)." href="#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1617_1">47</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Renny-Byfield, S., Rodgers-Melnick, E. &amp; Ross-Ibarra, J. Gene fractionation and function in the ancient subgenomes of maize. Mol. Biol. Evol. 34, 1825–1832 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1620">48</a></sup> and to be heritable through successive allopolyploid events<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Woodhouse, M. R. et al. Origin, inheritance, and gene regulatory consequences of genome dominance in polyploids. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 111, 5283–5288 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1624">49</a></sup>, and, as predicted<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Freeling, M. et al. Fractionation mutagenesis and similar consequences of mechanisms removing dispensable or less-expressed DNA in plants. Curr. Opin. Plant. Biol. 15, 131–139 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1629">22</a></sup>, it is not observed in ancient autopolyploids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Garsmeur, O. et al. Two evolutionarily distinct classes of paleopolyploidy. Mol. Biol. Evol. 31, 448–454 (2014)." href="#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1633">50</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhao, M., Zhang, B., Lisch, D. &amp; Ma, J. Patterns and consequences of subgenome differentiation provide insights into the nature of paleopolyploidy in plants. Plant Cell 29, 2974–2994 (2017)." href="#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1633_1">51</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Wendel, J. F., Lisch, D., Hu, G. &amp; Mason, A. S. The long and short of doubling down: polyploidy, epigenetics, and the temporal dynamics of genome fractionation. Curr. Opin. Genet. Dev. 49, 1–7 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1636">52</a></sup>. Moreover, subgenome expression dominance has recently been shown to occur instantly after interspecific hybridization and to increase over successive generations in monkeyflower<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Edger, P. P. et al. Subgenome dominance in an interspecific hybrid, synthetic allopolyploid, and a 140-year-old naturally established neo-allopolyploid monkeyflower. Plant Cell 29, 2150–2167 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1640">23</a></sup>. However, some allopolyploids, including <i>Capsella bursa-pastoris</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Douglas, G. M. et al. Hybrid origins and the earliest stages of diploidization in the highly successful recent polyploid Capsella bursa-pastoris. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 112, 2806–2811 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1646">53</a></sup> and <i>Cucurbita</i> species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Sun, H. et al. Karyotype stability and unbiased fractionation in the paleo-allotetraploid Cucurbita genomes. Mol. Plant 10, 1293–1306 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1654">54</a></sup>, do not exhibit subgenome dominance.</p><p>The emergence of a dominant subgenome may resolve various genetic and epigenetic conflicts that arise from the genomic merger of divergent diploid progenitor species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="McClintock, B. The significance of responses of the genome to challenge. Science 226, 792–801 (1984)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1661">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Osborn, T. C. et al. Understanding mechanisms of novel gene expression in polyploids. Trends Genet. 19, 141–147 (2003)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1664">55</a></sup>, including mismatches between transcriptional regulators and their target genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Bottani, S., Zabet, N. R., Wendel, J. F. &amp; Veitia, R. A. Gene expression dominance in allopolyploids: hypotheses and models. Trends Plant Sci. 23, 393–402 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1668">24</a></sup>. The mechanistic basis of subgenome dominance, at least in part, appears to be related to subgenome differences in the content and regulation of TEs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Freeling, M. et al. Fractionation mutagenesis and similar consequences of mechanisms removing dispensable or less-expressed DNA in plants. Curr. Opin. Plant. Biol. 15, 131–139 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1672">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Hollister, J. D. &amp; Gaut, B. S. Epigenetic silencing of transposable elements: a trade-off between reduced transposition and deleterious effects on neighboring gene expression. Genome Res. 19, 1419–1428 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1675">56</a></sup>. Gene expression levels are negatively correlated with the density of nearby TEs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Hollister, J. D. &amp; Gaut, B. S. Epigenetic silencing of transposable elements: a trade-off between reduced transposition and deleterious effects on neighboring gene expression. Genome Res. 19, 1419–1428 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1679">56</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM1">11</a>). Thus, the merger of subgenomes with different TE densities results in higher gene expression for the dominant homoeolog with fewer TEs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Freeling, M. et al. Fractionation mutagenesis and similar consequences of mechanisms removing dispensable or less-expressed DNA in plants. Curr. Opin. Plant. Biol. 15, 131–139 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1687">22</a></sup>. The abundance and distribution of TEs can be used to predict gene expression dominance and eventual gene loss at the individual homoeolog level<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Edger, P. P. et al. Subgenome dominance in an interspecific hybrid, synthetic allopolyploid, and a 140-year-old naturally established neo-allopolyploid monkeyflower. Plant Cell 29, 2150–2167 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1691">23</a></sup>.</p><p>Having identified the extant diploid relatives of octoploid strawberry, we used this information to investigate the evolutionary dynamics among the four subgenomes. We identified a dominant subgenome that was contributed by the <i>F. vesca</i> progenitor (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#Fig1">1</a>) and has retained 20.2% more protein-coding genes and 14.2% more lncRNA genes, and has overall 19.5% fewer TEs than the other homoeologous chromosomes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM1">9</a>). The overall TE densities near genes were also lowest for <i>F. vesca</i> compared with the other parental subgenomes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM1">11</a>). Furthermore, we identified ~40.6% more tandem gene duplications on homoeologous chromosomes of <i>F. vesca</i> compared with the other subgenomes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM1">9</a>). The <i>F. vesca</i> subgenome, compared with the other subgenomes, also contains a greater number of tandem gene arrays as well as larger average tandem-gene-array sizes on six of seven homoeologous chromosomes. These findings suggest that the dominant <i>F. vesca</i> subgenome, compared with the other three subgenomes, has been under stronger selective constraints to retain genes, including tandemly duplicated genes known to be biased toward gene families that encode important adaptive traits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Rizzon, C., Ponger, L. &amp; Gaut, B. S. Striking similarities in the genomic distribution of tandemly arrayed genes in Arabidopsis and rice. PLoS Comput. Biol. 2, e115 (2006)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1726">57</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Hanada, K., Zou, C., Lehti-Shiu, M. D., Shinozaki, K. &amp; Shiu, S.-H. Importance of lineage-specific expansion of plant tandem duplicates in the adaptive response to environmental stimuli. Plant Physiol. 148, 993–1003 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1729">58</a></sup>. For example, major disease-resistance genes in plants, including nucleotide-binding-site leucine-rich-repeat genes (NBS-LRRs), which are usually clustered in tandem arrays<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Qian, L.-H. et al. Distinct patterns of gene gain and loss: diverse evolutionary modes of NBS-encoding genes in three solanaceae crop species. G3 (Bethesda) 7, 1577–1585 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1733">59</a></sup>, are biased toward the dominant <i>F. vesca</i> subgenome (<i>χ</i>2 test, <i>P</i> &lt; 0.0001; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM1">12</a>).</p><p>Because strawberry production is threatened by several agriculturally important diseases, we analyzed, in greater depth, the major family of plant resistance (R) genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Meyers, B. C., Kozik, A., Griego, A., Kuang, H. &amp; Michelmore, R. W. Genome-wide analysis of NBS-LRR-encoding genes in Arabidopsis. Plant Cell 15, 809–834 (2003)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1753">60</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Dangl, J. L., Horvath, D. M. &amp; Staskawicz, B. J. Pivoting the plant immune system from dissection to deployment. Science 341, 746–751 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1756">61</a></sup>. Collectively, 423 NBS-LRR genes were identified, including 195 encoding an N-terminal coiled-coil (CC), 79 encoding toll interleukin 1 receptor (TIR), and 24 encoding resistance to powdery mildew 8 (RPW8) domains (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM1">12</a>). Recent work has demonstrated that many R proteins recognize pathogen effectors through integrated decoy domains<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Kroj, T., Chanclud, E., Michel-Romiti, C., Grand, X. &amp; Morel, J.-B. Integration of decoy domains derived from protein targets of pathogen effectors into plant immune receptors is widespread. New Phytol. 210, 618–626 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1763">62</a></sup>, and the <i>F. vesca</i> genome encodes 20 such protein models<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Sarris, P. F., Cevik, V., Dagdas, G., Jones, J. D. G. &amp; Krasileva, K. V. Comparative analysis of plant immune receptor architectures uncovers host proteins likely targeted by pathogens. BMC Biol. 14, 8 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1770">63</a></sup>. <i>Fragaria</i> <i>×</i> <i>ananassa</i> has a greatly expanded set of 105 diverse domains that are fused to the R-protein structures and have the potential to function as integrated decoys<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Kroj, T., Chanclud, E., Michel-Romiti, C., Grand, X. &amp; Morel, J.-B. Integration of decoy domains derived from protein targets of pathogen effectors into plant immune receptors is widespread. New Phytol. 210, 618–626 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1784">62</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM1">13</a> and Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM3">1</a>). Only a few resistance genes have been phenotypically identified in <i>Fragaria</i> × <i>ananassa</i>, but none have been functionally characterized<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Roach, J. A. et al. FaRXf1: a locus conferring resistance to angular leaf spot caused by Xanthomonas fragariae in octoploid strawberry. Theor. Appl. Genet. 129, 1191–1201 (2016)." href="#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1801">64</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Mangandi, J. et al. Pedigree-based analysis in a multiparental population of octoploid strawberry reveals QTL alleles conferring resistance to Phytophthora cactorum. G3 (Bethesda) 7, 1707–1719 (2017)." href="#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1801_1">65</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Pincot, D. D. A. et al. Genome-wide association mapping uncovers Fw1, a dominant gene conferring resistance to Fusarium wilt in strawberry. G3 (Bethesda) 8, 1817–1828 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1804">66</a></sup>. The annotated genome thus provides a framework for accelerating R-gene discovery, connecting phenotype to genotype, and pyramiding R genes by developing targeted, homoeolog-specific molecular markers.</p><p>Although chromosomes contributed by the <i>F. vesca</i> progenitor retained the most genes overall, certain regions on chromosomes from the other progenitor species retained higher numbers of ancestral genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#Fig1">1b</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM1">14</a>). Further analysis revealed that these regions are the products of homoeologous exchanges (HEs) or gene-conversion events<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Chalhoub, B. et al. Early allopolyploid evolution in the post-Neolithic Brassica napus oilseed genome. Science 345, 950–953 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1821">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Guo, H. et al. Extensive and biased intergenomic nonreciprocal DNA exchanges shaped a nascent polyploid genome, Gossypium (cotton). Genetics 197, 1153–1163 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1824">67</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Gaeta, R. T. &amp; Chris Pires, J. Homoeologous recombination in allopolyploids: the polyploid ratchet. New Phytol. 186, 18–28 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1827">68</a></sup> (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM1">15</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM1">16</a>). Notably, most HEs in octoploid strawberry involved replacements of the submissive homoeologs by corresponding regions of the dominant <i>F. vesca</i> subgenome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM1">10</a>). For example, our phylogenetic and comparative genomic analyses showed that HEs are 7.3× biased toward the <i>F. vesca</i> subgenome compared with <i>F. iinumae</i>, but they are not unidirectional as previously reported<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Tennessen, J. A., Govindarajulu, R., Ashman, T.-L. &amp; Liston, A. Evolutionary origins and dynamics of octoploid strawberry subgenomes revealed by dense targeted capture linkage maps. Genome Biol. Evol. 6, 3295–3313 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1850">3</a></sup>. HEs were even more biased toward the <i>F. vesca</i> subgenomes compared with the other two subgenomes (9.8× for <i>F. viridis</i> and 10.4× for <i>F. nipponica</i>). These analyses validate findings from a previous study in wild octoploid strawberry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Tennessen, J. A., Govindarajulu, R., Ashman, T.-L. &amp; Liston, A. Evolutionary origins and dynamics of octoploid strawberry subgenomes revealed by dense targeted capture linkage maps. Genome Biol. Evol. 6, 3295–3313 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1864">3</a></sup> and show that portions of the <i>F. iinumae</i> subgenome have been replaced with the <i>F. vesca</i> subgenome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#Fig1">1b</a>). Here, we identified HEs ranging in size from single genes to megabase-sized regions on chromosomes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM1">10</a>), findings similar to the patterns observed in other allopolyploids including <i>Brassica napus</i> (rapeseed)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Xiong, Z., Gaeta, R. T. &amp; Pires, J. C. Homoeologous shuffling and chromosome compensation maintain genome balance in resynthesized allopolyploid Brassica napus. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 7908–7913 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1884">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Chalhoub, B. et al. Early allopolyploid evolution in the post-Neolithic Brassica napus oilseed genome. Science 345, 950–953 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1887">28</a></sup>, <i>Gossypium hirsutum</i> (cotton)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Guo, H. et al. Extensive and biased intergenomic nonreciprocal DNA exchanges shaped a nascent polyploid genome, Gossypium (cotton). Genetics 197, 1153–1163 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1894">67</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Zhang, T. et al. Sequencing of allotetraploid cotton (Gossypium hirsutum L. acc. TM-1) provides a resource for fiber improvement. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 531–537 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1897">69</a></sup>, and bread wheat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="He, Z. et al. Extensive homoeologous genome exchanges in allopolyploid crops revealed by mRNAseq-based visualization. Plant. Biotechnol. J. 15, 594–604 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1902">70</a></sup>. The observed bias of HEs genome wide may be due to selection favoring the maintenance of proper network stoichiometry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Birchler, J. A., Bhadra, U., Bhadra, M. P. &amp; Auger, D. L. Dosage-dependent gene regulation in multicellular eukaryotes: implications for dosage compensation, aneuploid syndromes, and quantitative traits. Dev. Biol. 234, 275–288 (2001)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1906">71</a></sup> and altered dosage of certain gene products<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Bekaert, M., Edger, P. P., Pires, J. C. &amp; Conant, G. C. Two-phase resolution of polyploidy in the Arabidopsis metabolic network gives rise to relative and absolute dosage constraints. Plant Cell 23, 1719–1728 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1910">72</a></sup> during the establishment of the dominant subgenome. Interestingly, 32.6% of NBS-LRR genes encoded on the three submissive subgenomes are derived from HE with the <i>F. vesca</i> subgenome. This result suggests that although the <i>F. vesca</i> subgenome may also dominate disease resistance in strawberry, the maintained diversity of resistance mechanisms contributed by the other three diploid progenitors may also have been under selection.</p><p>Finally, we examined gene expression in diverse organs to test whether the dominant <i>F. vesca</i> subgenome is more highly expressed than the submissive genomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#Fig3">3</a>), as predicted by the subgenome-dominance hypothesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Freeling, M. et al. Fractionation mutagenesis and similar consequences of mechanisms removing dispensable or less-expressed DNA in plants. Curr. Opin. Plant. Biol. 15, 131–139 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1929">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Bird, K. A., VanBuren, R., Puzey, J. R. &amp; Edger, P. P. The causes and consequences of subgenome dominance in hybrids and recent polyploids. New Phytol. 220, 87–93 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1932">25</a></sup>. The density of TEs near genes was found to be negatively correlated with gene expression across all subgenomes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM1">11a</a>). Because HEs reshuffled and replaced homoeologs across each of the four parental chromosomes, only homoeolog pairs that had support for subgenome assignment were evaluated for subgenome expression dominance (that is, homoeolog expression bias). Our analyses revealed that the dominant <i>F. vesca</i> subgenome, which had the lowest overall TE densities near genes of all subgenomes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM1">11b</a>; Kolmogorov–Smirnov test, <i>P</i> &lt; 10<sup>−33</sup>), encodes more significantly dominantly expressed homoeologs than the other three submissive subgenomes combined (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#Fig3">3c</a>). This finding supports the hypothesis that subgenome expression dominance is influenced by overall TE-density differences between subgenomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Freeling, M. et al. Fractionation mutagenesis and similar consequences of mechanisms removing dispensable or less-expressed DNA in plants. Curr. Opin. Plant. Biol. 15, 131–139 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1954">22</a></sup>. At the individual homoeolog level, many dominantly expressed homoeologs were also contributed by one of the three submissive subgenomes. This observation was expected, given the variation in TE densities near homoeologs in each of the diploid progenitor genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Edger, P. P. et al. Subgenome dominance in an interspecific hybrid, synthetic allopolyploid, and a 140-year-old naturally established neo-allopolyploid monkeyflower. Plant Cell 29, 2150–2167 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1958">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Laricchia, K.
