M., Zdraljevic, S., Cook, D. E. &amp; Andersen, E. C. Natural variation in the distribution and abundance of transposable elements across the Caenorhabditis elegans Species. Mol. Biol. Evol. 34, 2187–2202 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d24836e1961">73</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Subgenome expression dominance."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Subgenome expression dominance.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0356-4/MediaObjects/41588_2019_356_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0356-4/MediaObjects/41588_2019_356_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="326" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Homoeolog expression bias (HEB) for all testable homoeolog pairs, shown in gray histograms. Testable homoeolog pairs (<i>n</i>) are those that could confidently be identified as homoeologous on the basis of synteny and assigned to a subgenome with phylogenetic support (&gt;80% bootstrap), and that had at least one read in each transcriptome dataset. Homoeolog pairs significantly biased toward the <i>F. vesca</i> homoeolog are shown in red, and pairs significantly biased toward the ‘other’ homoeolog from one of the other three diploid progenitors are shown in black.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Most HEs in octoploid strawberry resulted in the dominant <i>F. vesca</i> subgenome replacing the corresponding homoeologous regions of one of the submissive subgenomes. Thus, the observed homoeolog expression bias toward the <i>F. vesca</i> subgenome in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#Fig3">3</a> is an underestimate of transcriptome-wide expression dominance (68.7% of all transcripts). This bias has resulted in certain biological pathways being largely controlled by a single dominant subgenome. Our analyses revealed that certain metabolic pathways, including those that give rise to strawberry flavor, color, and aroma, are largely controlled by the dominant subgenome. For example <i>F. vesca</i> homoeologs in octoploid strawberry are responsible for 88.8% of the biosynthesis of anthocyanins, the metabolites responsible for the red pigments in ripening strawberry fruit; 89.2% of the biosynthesis of geranyl acetate, a terpene associated with fruit aroma; and 95.3% of the biosynthesis of fructose associated with sweetness (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM4">2</a>). Similar results have been found in allotetraploid <i>Brassica juncea</i>, in which many dominant homoeologs have been found to be related to glucosinolate biosynthesis and to show signs of positive selection<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Yang, J. et al. The genome sequence of allopolyploid Brassica juncea and analysis of differential homoeolog gene expression influencing selection. Nat. Genet. 48, 1225–1232 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2003">74</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec6" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec6-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec6">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec6-content"><p>We present what is, to our knowledge, the first chromosome-scale genome assembly for an octoploid strawberry—the highest-level polyploid genome of this quality assembled to date. Analysis of this genome allowed us to identify each of the diploid progenitor species, reconstruct the evolutionary history of the octoploid event, and investigate the evolution of a dominant subgenome. Our data support the hypothesis that subgenome dominance in an allopolyploid is established by TE-density differences near homoeologous genes in each of the diploid progenitor genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Freeling, M. et al. Fractionation mutagenesis and similar consequences of mechanisms removing dispensable or less-expressed DNA in plants. Curr. Opin. Plant. Biol. 15, 131–139 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2016">22</a></sup>. Furthermore, our results show that the <i>F. vesca</i> subgenome has increased in dominance over time by having retained significantly more ancestral genes and a greater number of tandemly duplicated genes than the other three subgenomes, and replaced large portions of the submissive subgenomes via homoeologous exchanges. These trends, combined with subgenome expression dominance, have resulted in many traits being largely controlled by a single dominant subgenome in octoploid strawberry. This finding is consistent with results from a recent report indicating that the dominant subgenome in maize contributes more to phenotypic variation than the submissive subgenome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Renny-Byfield, S., Rodgers-Melnick, E. &amp; Ross-Ibarra, J. Gene fractionation and function in the ancient subgenomes of maize. Mol. Biol. Evol. 34, 1825–1832 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2023">48</a></sup>. This reference genome should serve as a powerful platform for breeders to develop homoeolog-specific markers to track and leverage allelic diversity at target loci. Thus, we anticipate that this new reference genome, combined with insights into subgenome dominance, will greatly accelerate molecular breeding efforts in the cultivated garden strawberry.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">URLs</h3><p>Sequence Read Archive, <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/</a>; Google Maps, <a href="https://www.google.com/maps/">https://www.google.com/maps/</a>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Plant material</h3><p>The cultivar ‘Camarosa’ was selected because of its importance to the industry; historically, it has been one of the most widely grown short-day varieties worldwide, and it remains an important genotype in breeding programs. The haploid genome size (~813.4 Mb) was estimated through flow cytometry with four technical replicates at the Flow Cytometry Core at Benaroya Research Institute at Virginia Mason (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM5">3</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Genomic sequencing</h3><p>High-molecular-weight genomic DNA was isolated from young leaf tissue, after a 72-h dark treatment, through a modified nuclei-preparation method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Zhang, H.-B., Zhao, X., Ding, X., Paterson, A. H. &amp; Wing, R. A. Preparation of megabase-size DNA from plant nuclei. Plant J. 7, 175–184 (1995)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2124">75</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="VanBuren, R. et al. Single-molecule sequencing of the desiccation-tolerant grass Oropetium thomaeum. Nature 527, 508–511 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2127">76</a></sup>, and the quality was verified through pulsed-field gel electrophoresis. A total of five PacBio 20-kb libraries were generated with a SMRTbell Template Prep Kit (PacBio) and were sequenced with 67 SMRT cells on the PacBio RSII platform at the UC Davis DNA Sequencing Facility. A total of 67 Gb (~82.4×) of PacBio sequence data was generated with an <i>N</i>50 read length of 17,699 bp (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM5">3</a>). DNA fragments longer than 50 kb were used to construct a 10X Gemcode library with a Chromium instrument (10X Genomics) and sequenced on a HiSeqX system (Ilumina) with paired-end, 150-bp reads at the HudsonAlpha Institute for Biotechnology. A total of ~95 Gb (~117× fold coverage) of 10X Chromium library data was sequenced (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM3">1</a>). Finally, five size-selected Illumina genomic libraries ranging from 470 bp to 10 kb were constructed (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM3">1</a>). The ~470-bp and ~800-bp libraries were made with a Illumina TruSeq DNA PCR-free Sample Preparation V2 Kit. The two ~470-bp libraries were designed to produce ‘overlapping libraries’ after sequencing with paired-end, 265-bp reads on an Illumina Hiseq2500 system, producing ‘stitched’ reads of approximately 265 bp to 520 bp in length. To increase sequence diversity and depth, we constructed three separate mate-pair (MP) libraries with jumps of 2–5 kb, 5–7 kb, and 7–10 kb, with an Illumina Nextera Mate-Pair Sample Preparation Kit. The 800-bp library was sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq2500 system with paired-end, 160-bp reads, and the MP libraries were sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq4000 system with paired-end, 150-bp reads. A total of ~370 Gb (~455× fold coverage) of additional Illumina sequencing data was generated (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM3">1</a>). Illumina library construction and sequencing were conducted at the Roy J. Carver Biotechnology Center, University of Illinois at Urbana-Champaign.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Genome assembly</h3><p>The genome was assembled with the DeNovoMAGIC software platform (NRGene), a DeBruijn-graph-based assembler designed for higher polyploid, heterozygous and/or repetitive genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Avni, R. et al. Wild emmer genome architecture and diversity elucidate wheat evolution and domestication. Science 357, 93–97 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2155">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Luo, M.-C. et al. Genome sequence of the progenitor of the wheat D genome Aegilops tauschii. Nature 551, 498–502 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2158">77</a></sup>. The Chromium 10X data were used to phase haplotypes and support scaffold validation and further elongation of the phased scaffolds. Dovetail HiC libraries were prepared as described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Lieberman-Aiden, E. et al. Comprehensive mapping of long-range interactions reveals folding principles of the human genome. Science 326, 289–293 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2162">78</a></sup> and sequenced on an Illumina HiSeqX system with paired-end, 150-bp reads to ~401× sequence depth of the genome (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM1">2</a>). The initial de novo assembly, raw genomic reads, and Dovetail HiC library reads were used as input data for HiRise, a software pipeline designed specifically for using proximity-ligation data to scaffold genome assemblies to chromosome-length pseudomolecules<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Putnam, N. H. et al. Chromosome-scale shotgun assembly using an in vitro method for long-range linkage. Genome Res. 26, 342–350 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2169">79</a></sup>. After HiRise scaffolding, the sequences were gap filled with PacBio reads with PBJelly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="English, A. C. et al. Mind the gap: upgrading genomes with Pacific Biosciences RS long-read sequencing technology. PLoS One 7, e47768 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2173">33</a></sup>. Gaps filled with PacBio sequences were polished with Pilon (v 1.22)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS One 9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2178">80</a></sup> with Illumina paired-end data. Illumina reads were quality-trimmed with Trimmomatic<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Bolger, A. M., Lohse, M. &amp; Usadel, B. Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data. Bioinformatics 30, 2114–2120 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2182">81</a></sup> and aligned to the draft contigs with bowtie2 (v 2.3.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Fast gapped-read alignment with Bowtie 2. Nat. Methods 9, 357–359 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2186">82</a></sup> with default parameters. Parameters for Pilon were modified as follows: --flank 7, --K 49, and --mindepth 20. Pilon was run recursively three times, and there were minimal corrections in the third round, thus supporting accurate indel correction. A published genetic map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Davik, J. et al. A ddRAD based linkage map of the cultivated strawberry, Fragaria×ananassa. PLoS One 10, e0137746 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2190">34</a></sup> and syntenic analyses against the <i>F. vesca</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Edger, P. P. et al. Single-molecule sequencing and optical mapping yields an improved genome of woodland strawberry (Fragaria vesca) with chromosome-scale contiguity. Gigascience 7, 1–7 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2196">37</a></sup> genomes with SynMap within CoGe<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Lyons, E., Pedersen, B., Kane, J. &amp; Freeling, M. The value of nonmodel genomes and an example using SynMap within CoGe to dissect the hexaploidy that predates the Rosids. Trop. Plant Biol. 1, 181–190 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2201">83</a></sup> were used to identify any assembly errors and haplotype variants, and to assign homoeologous chromosomes sets. Additional assembly details and results are summarized in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM1">supplementary information</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Tissue collection, RNA library preparation, and sequencing</h3><p>Plant tissue samples (flower before anthesis, flower at anthesis, leaf collected during the day and at night, leaves treated with methyl jasmonate (30 min, 4 h, and 24 h after treatment), runner, and salt-treated and untreated roots) were collected from <i>Fragaria</i> × <i>ananassa</i> cultivar ‘Camarosa’ grown in a growth chamber and immediately flash frozen in liquid nitrogen. Leaf tissues were also collected from wild diploid species grown in a growth chamber for phylogenetic analyses (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM1">7</a>). Total RNA was isolated with a KingFisher Pure RNA Plant Kit (Thermo Fisher) and quantified with a Qubit 3 fluorometer (Thermo Fisher). RNA libraries were prepared with the KAPA mRNA HyperPrep Kit protocol (KAPA Biosystems). All samples were submitted to the Michigan State University Research Technology Support Facility Genomics core and sequenced with paired-end, 150-bp reads on an Illumina HiSeq 4000 system.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Transcriptome assembly and translation</h3><p>Reads were cleaned with Trimmomatic v 0.32 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Bolger, A. M., Lohse, M. &amp; Usadel, B. Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data. Bioinformatics 30, 2114–2120 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2234">81</a></sup>) with adaptor trimming for TruSeq3 paired-end reads with a 1-bp mismatch, a palindrome clip threshold of 30, and a simple clip threshold of 10. Reads were then filtered on the basis of an average phred score calculated from a sliding window of 10 bp with a minimum threshold of 20 (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM6">4</a>). The quality of trimmed reads was assessed afterward with FastQC<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Leggett, R. M., Ramirez-Gonzalez, R. H., Clavijo, B. J., Waite, D. &amp; Davey, R. P. Sequencing quality assessment tools to enable data-driven informatics for high throughput genomics. Front. Genet. 4, 288 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2241">84</a></sup>. Genome-guided and de novo transcriptome assemblies were generated with Trinity v 2.2.0 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="Grabherr, M. G. et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 644–652 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2245">85</a></sup>) for the genome annotation/expression and phylogenetic analyses, respectively. For genome annotation and expression analyses, reads were aligned to the <i>Fragaria</i> × <i>ananassa</i> cultivar ‘Camarosa’ genome with STAR v 2.5.3a<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 86" title="Dobin, A. &amp; Gingeras, T. R. Mapping RNA-seq reads with STAR. Curr. Protoc. Bioinformatics. 51, 11.14.1–11.14.19 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR86" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2256">86</a></sup> with default options, except for --alignIntronMax, which was set to 10000. For genome annotation, the coordinate-sorted BAM output files from STAR were used for the genome-guided transcriptome assembly, and name-sorted SAM files were used for gene expression analysis (HTSeq in section 3). For the diploid species libraries used in the phylogenetic analyses, because transcriptome libraries were generated with a stranded method, the ‘SS_lib_type’ parameter with ‘RF’ option was used in the assembly. In addition, reads were normalized to a maximum read coverage of 100 with ‘normalize_max_read_cov’ in Trinity. The normalization option, which decreases the quantity of input reads for highly expressed genes, was used to improve assembly efficiency<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="Haas, B. J. et al. De novo transcript sequence reconstruction from RNA-seq using the Trinity platform for reference generation and analysis. Nat. Protoc. 8, 1494–1512 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2260">87</a></sup>. For homoeolog expression bias (HEB) analyses (described in the section below), counts of uniquely mapping reads were generated with HTSeq v 0.6.1 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 88" title="Anders, S., Pyl, P. T. &amp; Huber, W. HTSeq: a Python framework to work with high-throughput sequencing data. Bioinformatics 31, 166–169 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR88" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2264">88</a></sup>) with default options of htseq-count, except for feature type, which was set to ‘gene’ for all RNA-seq datasets of ‘Camarosa’. The fragments per kilobase per million reads mapped (FPKM) values were derived with the standard formula for FPKM = (read count/’per million’ scaling factor)/gene length in kilobases. For phylogenetic analysis, according to McKain et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="McKain, M. R. et al. A phylogenomic assessment of ancient polyploidy and genome evolution across the Poales. Genome Biol. Evol. 8, 1150–1164 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2268">89</a></sup>, reads were aligned to the assembled transcripts with bowtie v 1.1.0 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 90" title="Langmead, B., Trapnell, C., Pop, M. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Ultrafast and memory-efficient alignment of short DNA sequences to the human genome. Genome. Biol. 10, R25 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR90" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2272">90</a></sup>), and transcript abundance was estimated with RSEM v 1.2.29 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 91" title="Li, B. &amp; Dewey, C. N. RSEM: accurate transcript quantification from RNA-Seq data with or without a reference genome. BMC Bioinformatics 12, 323 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR91" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2277">91</a></sup>) through the align_and_estimate_abundance.pl script packaged with Trinity. Transcripts were filtered by FPKM, an output from the aforementioned Perl script, with a minimum threshold of 1.0% of fragments per isoform mapped, as implemented in the filter_fasta_by_rsem_values.pl script. Filtered transcripts were BLASTed against the <i>Fragaria vesca</i> v 2.01 coding sequences with TBLASTX with a minimum <i>e</i> value of 1 × 10<sup>–10</sup>. The RefTrans package (see URLs) was used to translate assembled transcripts by filtering BLAST hits to identify the best hit with at least 75% bidirectional overlap between the transcript and <i>F. vesca</i> coding sequences. Best hits were used to guide translations with GeneWise (Wise2 v 2.2.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 92" title="Birney, E., Clamp, M. &amp; Durbin, R. GeneWise and Genomewise. Genome Res. 14, 988–995 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR92" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2292">92</a></sup>. The longest translations were used in downstream analyses.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Gene annotation</h3><p>The genome was annotated with the MAKER-P annotation pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Campbell, M. S. et al. MAKER-P: a tool kit for the rapid creation, management, and quality control of plant genome annotations. Plant Physiol. 164, 513–524 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2304">36</a></sup>. Protein sequences (Araport11 and UniprotKB plant database), expressed sequence tags (NCBI), and ten mRNA-seq datasets (described below) and additional RNA-seq data for <i>Fragaria</i> × <i>ananassa</i> downloaded from NCBI-SRA (BioProject <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA394190">PRJNA394190</a>; red ripening fruit) were used as evidence during annotation. The RNA-seq datasets were assembled into transcripts through the StringTie genome-guided approach<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 93" title="Pertea, M. et al. StringTie enables improved reconstruction of a transcriptome from RNA-seq reads. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 290–295 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR93" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2318">93</a></sup>. A custom repeat library (‘Repeat annotation’ section below) and MAKER repeat library<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 94" title="Cantarel, B. L. et al. MAKER: an easy-to-use annotation pipeline designed for emerging model organism genomes. Genome Res. 18, 188–196 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR94" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2323">94</a></sup> were used for genome masking. Ab initio gene prediction was performed with the gene predictors SNAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 95" title="Korf, I. Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinformatics 5, 59 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR95" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2327">95</a></sup> and Augustus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 96" title="Stanke, M. &amp; Waack, S. Gene prediction with a hidden Markov model and a new intron submodel. Bioinformatics 19 (Suppl. 2), ii215–ii225 (2003)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR96" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2331">96</a></sup>, which were previously iteratively trained for <i>F. vesca</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Edger, P. P. et al. Single-molecule sequencing and optical mapping yields an improved genome of woodland strawberry (Fragaria vesca) with chromosome-scale contiguity. Gigascience 7, 1–7 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2337">37</a></sup>. During annotation, gene models with annotation edit distance &lt;1.0 were included in the MAKER gene set and scanned for the presence of protein domains. The predicted gene models were further filtered to remove those with TE-related domains. Briefly, the protein-coding genes were searched (BLASTp, <i>e</i> = 10<sup>–10</sup>) against a transposase database from a previous study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Campbell, M. S. et al. MAKER-P: a tool kit for the rapid creation, management, and quality control of plant genome annotations. Plant Physiol. 164, 513–524 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2347">36</a></sup>, and if more than 50% of gene length aligned to the transposases, the gene was removed from the gene set. However, if 60% or more of the amino acid matches were due to only three individual amino acids, the alignment was considered to be caused by low complexity and was excluded. In addition, to assess whether core plant genes were annotated, the gene set was searched against the BUSCO v 2 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Simão, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2351">35</a></sup>) plant dataset (embryophyta_odb9). lncRNAs, including long intergenic noncoding RNAs, antisense overlapping transcripts, and sense overlapping transcripts, were identified with the Evolinc lncRNA-discovery pipeline (v 1.5.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 97" title="Nelson, A. D. L. et al. Evolinc: a tool for the identification and evolutionary comparison of long intergenic non-coding RNAs. Front. Genet. 8, 52 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR97" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2355">97</a></sup>. Transcripts with fewer than three reads per base pair were discarded. Putative lncRNAs with similarity (BLASTn <i>e</i> value &lt;1 × 10<sup>10</sup>) to known TEs or rFAM’s catalog (v 13.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 98" title="Kalvari, I. et al. Rfam 13.0: shifting to a genome-centric resource for non-coding RNA families. Nucleic Acids Res. 46, D335–D342 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR98" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2365">98</a></sup> of housekeeping RNAs were removed.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Repeat annotation</h3><p>The <i>Fragaria</i> × <i>ananassa</i> genome was searched for LTR-RTs with LTRharvest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 99" title="Ellinghaus, D., Kurtz, S. &amp; Willhoeft, U. LTRharvest, an efficient and flexible software for de novo detection of LTR retrotransposons. BMC Bioinformatics 9, 18 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR99" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2383">99</a></sup> with parameters ‘-minlenltr 100 -maxlenltr 7000 -mintsd 4 -maxtsd 6 -motif TGCA -motifmis 1 -similar 85 -vic 10 -seed 20 -seqids yes’ and LTR_finder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 100" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265–W268 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR100" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2387">100</a></sup> with parameters ‘-D 15000 -d 1000 -L 7000 -l 100 -p 20 -M 0.9’. The identified LTR-RT candidates were filtered with LTR_retriever<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 101" title="Ou, S. &amp; Jiang, N. LTR_retriever: a highly accurate and sensitive program for identification of long terminal repeat retrotransposons. Plant Physiol. 176, 1410–1422 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR101" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2391">101</a></sup> with default parameters. Miniature inverted TEs (MITEs) were identified with MITE-Hunter<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 102" title="Han, Y. &amp; Wessler, S. R. MITE-Hunter: a program for discovering miniature inverted-repeat transposable elements from genomic sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 38, e199 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR102" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2396">102</a></sup>. Candidate MITEs were manually checked for TSD and TIR, which were used for superfamily classification. Those with ambiguous TSD and TIR were classified as unknowns. The <i>Fragaria</i> × <i>ananassa</i> genome was then masked with both MITE and LTR libraries through Repeatmasker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 103" title="Tarailo-Graovac, M. &amp; Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr. Protoc. Bioinformatics. 25, 4.10 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR103" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2406">103</a></sup> (see URLs), and other repetitive elements were identified with Repeatmodeler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 104" title="Bao, L. &amp; Liu, Z. in Bzioinformatics in Aquaculture (ed. Liu, Z. J.) 8, 86–97 (Wiley, Hoboken, NJ, USA, 2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR104" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2410">104</a></sup> (see URLs). The repeats were then grouped into two categories: sequences of known identity and sequences of unknown identity. The latter were then searched against the transposase database, and if they had a match, they were included in the TE library. The library was further filtered with ProtExcluder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Campbell, M. S. et al. MAKER-P: a tool kit for the rapid creation, management, and quality control of plant genome annotations. Plant Physiol. 164, 513–524 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2414">36</a></sup> and an in-house Perl script to exclude gene fragments. The final TE library was used to annotate the <i>Fragaria</i> × <i>ananassa</i> genome with RepeatMasker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 103" title="Tarailo-Graovac, M. &amp; Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr. Protoc. Bioinformatics. 25, 4.10 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR103" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2425">103</a></sup> with parameters ‘-q -no_is -norna -nolow -div 40’. Annotation results were summarized with the ‘famcoverage.pl’ script from the LTR-retriever package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 101" title="Ou, S. &amp; Jiang, N. LTR_retriever: a highly accurate and sensitive program for identification of long terminal repeat retrotransposons. Plant Physiol. 176, 1410–1422 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR101" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2429">101</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Organellar genome annotation</h3><p>The chloroplast genome was annotated with Verdant, a web-based software suite specifically designed for plant chloroplast genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 105" title="McKain, M. R., Hartsock, R. H., Wohl, M. M. &amp; Kellogg, E. A. Verdant: automated annotation, alignment and phylogenetic analysis of whole chloroplast genomes. Bioinformatics 33, 130–132 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR105" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2441">105</a></sup>. Automated annotation of protein-coding genes, tRNAs, and rRNAs was completed with annoBTD (see URLs). Five Rosaceae plastomes in the Verdant database were selected as a reference for annotation, including the <i>Fragaria vesca</i> ‘Hawaii 4’ chloroplast genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Edger, P. P. et al. Single-molecule sequencing and optical mapping yields an improved genome of woodland strawberry (Fragaria vesca) with chromosome-scale contiguity. Gigascience 7, 1–7 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2448">37</a></sup>. The previously identified ORFs were BLASTed against the reference genomes with TBLASTX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 106" title="Camacho, C. et al. BLAST+: architecture and applications. BMC Bioinformatics 10, 421 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR106" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2452">106</a></sup> with an <i>e</i>-value cutoff of 0.1 and a cutoff of 50% identity between references and high-scoring segment pairs. The best reference for each ORF was used for annotation. An optimized BLASTN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 106" title="Camacho, C. et al. BLAST+: architecture and applications. BMC Bioinformatics 10, 421 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR106" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2460">106</a></sup> was used to identify and annotate tRNAs and rRNAs on the basis of reference genomes. The best-scoring references were used to annotate the RNA. Finally, the boundaries of each feature was identified on the basis of the sequence and positional information for the orthologous features from the five reference chloroplast genomes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM1">5</a>). The mitochondrial genome was annotated with the webserver for Mitofy (see URLs), a program designed to annotate the genes and tRNAs in the mitochondrial genomes of seed plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 107" title="Alverson, A. J. et al. Insights into the evolution of mitochondrial genome size from complete sequences of Citrullus lanatus and Cucurbita pepo (Cucurbitaceae). Mol. Biol. Evol. 27, 1436–1448 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR107" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2467">107</a></sup>. Mitofy uses NCBI-BLASTX to annotated genes on the basis of databases of 41 protein-coding genes and uses NCBI-BLASTN and tRNAscan-SE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 108" title="Lowe, T. M. &amp; Eddy, S. R. tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 955–964 (1997)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR108" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2471">108</a></sup> to annotate tRNAs and rRNAs on the basis of databases of 27 tRNAs and 3 rRNAs found in seed-plant mitochondrial plant genomes. The annotated plastid and mitochondrial genomes have been deposited in Dryad (see URLs).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Synteny and comparative genomics</h3><p>The ‘Camarosa’ and <i>F. vesca</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Edger, P. P. et al. Single-molecule sequencing and optical mapping yields an improved genome of woodland strawberry (Fragaria vesca) with chromosome-scale contiguity. Gigascience 7, 1–7 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2485">37</a></sup> genomes were aligned in CoGe’s SynMap program with LAST<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Lyons, E., Pedersen, B., Kane, J. &amp; Freeling, M. The value of nonmodel genomes and an example using SynMap within CoGe to dissect the hexaploidy that predates the Rosids. Trop. Plant Biol. 1, 181–190 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2489">83</a></sup>. The maximum distance between two matches was set to 20 genes, and the minimum number of aligned pairs was set to ten genes. Neighboring syntenic blocks were merged with ‘Quota Align Merge’<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 109" title="Tang, H. et al. Screening synteny blocks in pairwise genome comparisons through integer programming. BMC Bioinformatics 12, 102 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR109" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2493">109</a></sup>, with the maximum distance between two blocks set to 40 genes. Syntenic depth was calculated with ‘Quota Align’, and the ratio of coverage depth for <i>F. vesca</i> to <i>F. ananassa</i> gene was set to 1:4. Tandemly duplicated genes were identified and filtered from CoGe outputs with a max distance of ten genes. Fractionation bias was then calculated, with the maximum query chromosomes set to 28 and the maximum target chromosomes set to seven. The analyses can be regenerated with CoGe (see URLs). The two genomes were also aligned with MUMmer v 3.2 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 110" title="Kurtz, S. et al. Versatile and open software for comparing large genomes. Genome. Biol. 5, R12 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR110" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2504">110</a></sup>) to identify homoeologous exchanges (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM1">10</a>) with parameters (nucmer --maxmatch -l 80 -c 200) and visualized with dotPlotly (see URLs).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Phylogenetic analyses</h3><p>Translated transcriptomes and whole-genome protein-coding genes for <i>Fragaria</i> × <i>ananassa</i>, <i>F. vesca</i> v 2.01, <i>A. thaliana</i> TAIR10 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 111" title="Lamesch, P. et al. The Arabidopsis Information Resource (TAIR): improved gene annotation and new tools. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, D1202–D1210 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR111" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2531">111</a></sup>), and <i>Malus domestica</i> v 1.0 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 112" title="Velasco, R. et al. The genome of the domesticated apple (Malus × domestica Borkh.). Nat. Genet. 42, 833–839 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR112" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2539">112</a></sup>) (Phytozome v 12)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 113" title="Goodstein, D. M. et al. Phytozome: a comparative platform for green plant genomics. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, D1178–D1186 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR113" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2543">113</a></sup> were orthogrouped with Orthofinder v 0.3 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 114" title="Emms, D. M. &amp; Kelly, S. OrthoFinder: solving fundamental biases in whole genome comparisons dramatically improves orthogroup inference accuracy. Genome. Biol. 16, 157 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR114" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2547">114</a></sup>) with Diamond v 0.8.36 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 115" title="Buchfink, B., Xie, C. &amp; Huson, D. H. Fast and sensitive protein alignment using DIAMOND. Nat. Methods 12, 59–60 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR115" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2551">115</a></sup>) for similarity searches. Orthogroups were filtered so that a minimum of five unique accessions were present. Coding sequences and amino acid translations were separated into orthogroup-specific FASTA files. Amino acid sequences were aligned with MAFFT v 7.215 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 116" title="Katoh, K. &amp; Standley, D. M. MAFFT multiple sequence alignment software version 7: improvements in performance and usability. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 772–780 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR116" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2555">116</a></sup>) with the ‘auto’ parameter, and PAL2NAL v 14 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 117" title="Suyama, M., Torrents, D. &amp; Bork, P. PAL2NAL: robust conversion of protein sequence alignments into the corresponding codon alignments. Nucleic Acids Res. 34, W609–W612 (2006)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR117" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2560">117</a></sup>) was used under default parameters to create a codon alignment from MAFFT-aligned amino acids. Codon alignments were filtered by removal of alignment columns with 90% or more gaps and transcripts with unaligned lengths less than 30% of the alignment length, with scripts provided with McKain et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="McKain, M. R. et al. A phylogenomic assessment of ancient polyploidy and genome evolution across the Poales. Genome Biol. Evol. 8, 1150–1164 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2564">89</a></sup>. Orthogroup trees were reconstructed with RAxML v 8.0.6 with 500 bootstrap replicates under the GTR + gamma evolutionary model. All 108,087 protein-coding genes from the <i>F</i>. x <i>ananassa</i> ‘Camarosa’ genome were used in the initial orthogrouping. After the filtering of orthogroups with fewer than five taxa, 51,737 ‘Camarosa’ genes remained in 8,405 gene trees. A total of 19,302 unique loci identified in large syntenic blocks forming 18,839 paralogous pairs were used to assess the evolutionary history of the subgenomes. Outgroups were chosen from either <i>A. thaliana</i> or <i>M. domestica</i>, with preference given to <i>A. thaliana</i> as an outgroup. To assess the evolutionary history of octoploid strawberry’s subgenomes, a novel tree-searching algorithm was developed called ‘phylogenetic identification of subgenomes’ (PhyDS; see URLs). The only parameters needed for PhyDS are a list of taxa, if any, to ignore in the gene trees and a minimum bootstrap value to set the threshold for acceptable subtrees. In this analysis, only genes from the ‘Camarosa’ genome were ignored (that is, PhyDS did not stop when it encountered an Fxa gene other than a sister paralog) to identify each of the diploid progenitors of octoploid strawberry. Results from varying bootstrap support cutoffs are provided. These homoeologs were than mapped back to each of the assembled chromosomes and, on the basis of their relative frequencies, used to assign each chromosome to a diploid progenitor species (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM1">8</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Gene expression analyses</h3><p>HEB was assessed with the likelihood-ratio tests described in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Edger, P. P. et al. Subgenome dominance in an interspecific hybrid, synthetic allopolyploid, and a 140-year-old naturally established neo-allopolyploid monkeyflower. Plant Cell 29, 2150–2167 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2596">23</a></sup>, by analysis of the anther, root, and leaf transcriptome data. This test consists of a set of three nested hypotheses. The null hypothesis, <i>H</i><sub>0</sub>, is that the homoeologs are expressed at equal levels after normalization for gene length and sequencing depth. The first alternative hypothesis, <i>H</i><sub>1</sub>, is that one of the homoeologs is more highly expressed in all tissues, such that the difference can be explained by a single scaling factor. The second alternative hypothesis, <i>H</i><sub>2</sub>, is that the homoeologs are expressed unequally and inconsistently across the three tissues. Homoeolog pairs for which <i>H</i><sub>0</sub> can be rejected for <i>H</i><sub>1</sub>, but <i>H</i><sub>1</sub> cannot be rejected for <i>H</i><sub>2</sub>, are therefore cases in which one of the homoeologs appears to be up- or downregulated consistently throughout the organism. For the first test, the Benjamini–Hochberg<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 118" title="Benjamini, Y. &amp; Hochberg, Y. Controlling the false discovery rate: a practical and powerful approach to multiple testing. J. R. Stat. Soc. Series B Stat. Methodol. 57, 289–300 (1995)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR118" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2630">118</a></sup> correction for multiple testing was applied. For the second test, because the question was being unable to reject a hypothesis, no correction was made. Both tests used a 1% significance level. Pairwise genomic alignments, described above, were used to identify homoeologs for each of the subgenomes, retained duplicate genes from tandem duplications, and orthologous genes to <i>A. thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 111" title="Lamesch, P. et al. The Arabidopsis Information Resource (TAIR): improved gene annotation and new tools. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, D1202–D1210 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR111" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2636">111</a></sup>, on the basis of ortholog assignments in <i>F. vesca</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Edger, P. P. et al. Single-molecule sequencing and optical mapping yields an improved genome of woodland strawberry (Fragaria vesca) with chromosome-scale contiguity. Gigascience 7, 1–7 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2642">37</a></sup>. Thes complete list of <i>Fragaria</i>–<i>Arabidopsis</i> orthologs was then filtered to genes with functional data in the AraGEM <i>Arabidopsis</i> metabolic<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Bekaert, M., Edger, P. P., Pires, J. C. &amp; Conant, G. C. Two-phase resolution of polyploidy in the Arabidopsis metabolic network gives rise to relative and absolute dosage constraints. Plant Cell 23, 1719–1728 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2656">72</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 119" title="de Oliveira Dal’Molin, C. G., Quek, L.-E., Palfreyman, R. W., Brumbley, S. M. &amp; Nielsen, L. K. AraGEM, a genome-scale reconstruction of the primary metabolic network in. Arabidopsis. Plant Physiol. 152, 579–589 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR119" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2659">119</a></sup> and STRING global protein interaction network<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 120" title="Szklarczyk, D. et al. The STRING database in 2017: quality-controlled protein-protein association networks, made broadly accessible. Nucleic Acids Res. 45, D362–D368 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR120" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2663">120</a></sup>. These gene lists were used to investigate subgenome- and pathway-level-specific expression in fruit with an available transcriptome dataset in NCBI-SRA (BioProject <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA394190">PRJNA394190</a>) (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM4">2</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Analysis of disease-resistance-gene familie</h3><p>NBS-LRR genes were detected with HMMER v 3.1 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 121" title="Eddy, S. R. Accelerated profile HMM searches. PLoS Comput. Biol. 7, e1002195 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR121" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2682">121</a></sup>) with default settings, by searching the protein sequences of the <i>Fragaria</i> × <i>ananassa</i> genome against the raw hidden Markov model for the NB-ARC-domain family downloaded from Pfam (family ID PF00931)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 122" title="Finn, R. D. et al. The Pfam protein families database: towards a more sustainable future. Nucleic Acids Res. 44 (D1), D279–D285 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR122" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2692">122</a></sup>. Only genes identified by both HMMER and BLAST were used for subsequent analysis. TIR subdomains were detected with PfamScan on default settings by searching the identified NB-ARC genes against the Pfam-A hidden Markov model. The 423 Fxa NB-ARC-domain-containing proteins were batch-searched in the NCBI Conserved Domain Database (see URLs)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 123" title="Marchler-Bauer, A. et al. CDD: NCBI’s conserved domain database. Nucleic Acids Res. 43, D222–D226 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR123" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2696">123</a></sup> and Pfam database (see URLs). Results from the CD database were used to assign the gene models that contained CC, TIR, RPW8, or ‘other’ (none of the three established N-terminal domains); gene models were further mapped onto the assembled octoploid genome to assign positions (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM1">12</a>). The CD results were then filtered to remove established R-gene domains (CC, TIR, RPW8, LRR, and NB-ARC), thus resulting in a list of potential integrated domains (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM3">1</a>). Eight Fxa proteins with predicted Sec7/ADP-ribosylation-factor and G-nucleotide-exchange-factor domains were aligned by ClustalW and FastME 2.0 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 124" title="Lefort, V., Desper, R. &amp; Gascuel, O. FastME 2.0: a comprehensive, accurate, and fast distance-based phylogeny inference program. Mol. Biol. Evol. 32, 2798–2800 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR124" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2707">124</a></sup>), and their illustrated domain organization is displayed in Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM1">13</a>. The full protein sequences of the 423 Fxa NB-ARC-domain-containing proteins were aligned with MUSCLE v 3.8.31 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 125" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792–1797 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR125" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2714">125</a></sup>) under default settings. This alignment was trimmed with trimAl v 1.4.rev22 build 2015-05-21 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 126" title="Capella-Gutiérrez, S., Silla-Martínez, J. M. &amp; Gabaldón, T. trimAl: a tool for automated alignment trimming in large-scale phylogenetic analyses. Bioinformatics 25, 1972–1973 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR126" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2718">126</a></sup>) under default settings. An unrooted maximum-likelihood tree was constructed with RAxML v 8.2.11 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 127" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML version 8: a tool for phylogenetic analysis and post-analysis of large phylogenies. Bioinformatics 30, 1312–1313 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR127" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2723">127</a></sup>) with the PROTGAMMA substitution model. The tree was visualized with the APE package v 4.1 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 128" title="Paradis, E., Claude, J. &amp; Strimmer, K. APE: analyses of phylogenetics and evolution in R language. Bioinformatics 20, (289–290 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR128" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2727">128</a></sup>) in R v 3.3.3 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 129" title="Ihaka, R. &amp; Gentleman, R. R. A language for data analysis and graphics. J. Comput. Graph. Stat. 5, 299–314 (1996)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#ref-CR129" id="ref-link-section-d24836e2731">129</a></sup>) (see URLs).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Statistical analysis</h3><p>The comparison of homoeolog-expression abundance between the dominant subgenome and the three submissive subgenomes was carried out with a likelihood-ratio test and combined with Benjamini–Hochberg correction for multiple testing with a 1% significance level. The Kolmogorov–Smirnov test was used to determine which subgenome had the lowest-overall TE densities near genes. The <i>χ</i><sup>2</sup> test, with three degrees of freedom, was used to analyze the subgenome bias of disease-resistance genes. Bootstrapping, with 500 replicates under the GTR + gamma evolutionary model, was used to assess node support in trees generated by phylogenetic analyses.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Reporting Summary</h3><p>Further information on research design is available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0356-4#MOESM2">Nature Research Reporting Summary</a> linked to this article.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>The genome assembly, annotation files, alignments, and phylogenetic trees are available on Dryad (see URLs). Custom software for running PhyDS phylogenetic analyses is available on GitHub (see URLs). The genome assembly and annotation files are also available on the Genome Database for Rosaceae (GDR; see URLs) and the CyVerse CoGe platform (see URLs). ‘Camarosa’ clones are available from most strawberry nurseries. The raw sequence data are available in the Sequence Read Archive under NCBI BioProject <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA508389">PRJNA508389</a> (see URLs).</p>

M.C., G.S.C., and C.B.A. collected the samples and extracted DNA and RNA. P.P.E., S.J.K., M.C., C.B.A., K.B., and T.S. coordinated the Illumina and PacBio sequencing. P.P.E., T.J.P., R.V., M.A.H., C.M.W., G.B.-Z., A.B., K.B., T.S., L.S., E.I.A., and J.P.M. assembled the nuclear and organellar genomes. M.C., K.L.C., A.D.L.N., S.O., N.J., E.I.A. and J.P.M. annotated the nuclear and organellar genomes. M.R.K. and P.P.E. performed phylogenetic analyses. P.P.E., T.J.P., R.V., M.A.H., M.F. and E.L. ran comparative genomic analyses. A.E.Y., K.A.B., N.P., P.P.E., and S.J.K. identified disease-resistance genes. P.P.E., R.D.S., S.J.T., J.R.P., and A.D.L.N. performed gene expression analyses. M.C., M.R.M., M.A.H., S.J.K., and P.P.E. were in charge of data submission. P.P.E. wrote the manuscript draft, and all coauthors reviewed and revised the manuscript.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding authors</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to

                        class="js-separator">&nbsp;&amp;&nbsp;</span><span>Binghua Wu</span></li></ul>
              
              <p class="c-article-further-reading__journal-title" id="journal-name-5">
                <i>Phytochemistry</i>
                (2021)
              </p>
            </li>
          
        </ul>
      </div>
    </div>
    </section>
